♠ Original name ♣ Roman Kingdom ♥
♠ Alternative names ♣ Rome; Kingdom of Rome; Regal Period ♥
♠ Peak Date ♣ 535 BCE ♥ Under the last king, Lucius Tarquinius Superbus. Represented by the building of the Temple of Jupiter. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_1_ItRomRg] Or after the reforms of Servius Tullius (578-535 BC) - since the last king was overthrown.
♠ Duration ♣ 716-509 BCE ♥In terms of earliest habitation, there is archaeological evidence for the permanent occupation of Rome “centuries before 754 BCE” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_ItRomRg] perhaps from 1000 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_ItRomRg] In 2014, "The daily Il Messagero quoted Patrizia Fortini, the archaeologist responsible for the Forum, as saying that a wall constructed well before the city's traditional founding date had been unearthed." Examination of ceramic material found beside the wall suggested a date "between the 9th century and the beginning of the 8th century." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]First king of Rome may be Numa Pompilus (716-674 BCE) and a palace found on Palatine Hill dates to the late 8th century BCE. But was Numa Pompilus really the first king? Romulus, the official founder, was mythical but could be representative of an earlier date (753 BCE?).[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Hellenisation of Latium began in the 8th Century. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_ItRomRg] This timeframe (730-580 BCE) was an “orientalising period” marked by increasing social stratification shown by burial evidence of wealth, armour and chariots. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Not a peak date but a notable moment: the period of rule under Etruscan monarchs, beginning with Lucius Tarquinius Priscus from 616 BCE, saw a step-up in hierarchization of the Roman polity, and dates the moment when two groups known as patricians and plebians became more distinguishable. The first paving of the Roman Forum (meeting-place, market and civic centre) occurred around 625-575 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_ItRomRg] The first senate building, the Curia Hostilia, existed from about 600 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_ItRomRg] Monumental architecture was present from the end of the 7th Century[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_ItRomRg] and included a sanctuary constructed in 580 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_ItRomRg] Etruscan monarchs were responsible for large building projects such as the Cloaca Maxima (sewer system), the Circus Maximus and in 535 BCE the last king Lucius Tarquinius Superbus (another Etruscan) built the Temple of Jupiter.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Last king is Lucius Tarquinius Superbus (534-509 BCE). He was expelled from Rome by the aristocrats who set up Rome as a Republic. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_ItRomRg]

♠ Capital ♣ Rome ♥
♠ Language ♣ Latin ♥
♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥The Regal Period refers to the period at the end of the Iron Age during which Rome developed as a uncleared settlement in the heart of Latium (modern-day Lazio), ruled over by 'Etruscan kings'. Although there is archaeological evidence for the permanent occupation of Rome from the Iron Age 'centuries before' the city's mythic foundation date of 754 BCE,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_ItRomRg] perhaps from as early as 1000 BCE,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_ItRomRg] we have chosen to begin this polity in 716, with the traditional death date of the city's legendary founder Romulus.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_ItRomRg] The city prospered during this time, which saw the development of many of the institutions - political administration, legal system, religious practices - characteristic of the later Roman Republic. The last king, Lucius Tarquinius Superbus (534-509 BCE), was expelled from Rome for his and his family's tyrannical excesses. In his place, the leading Roman elites established an aristocratic city-state, ushering in the Republican period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]By end of the Regal Period, Rome was a well-developed city-state, boasting civic infrastructure (marsh drainage, roads), political institutions (assemblies, Senate), monuments (temples), and a powerful military. It held at least one third of the area of Latium vetus (Old Latium)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_ItRomRg] and had a population of 20,000-50,000 people.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]According to legend, Rome became a city when the eponymous founder Romulus slew his twin brother Remus, both outcasts from a nearby Latium settlement, in a contest over where to found their new city. Numa Pompilius (r. 715-673 BCE), of Sabine origin, was the next king. Pompilius is traditionally credited with establishing 'all the major religious institutions of the state, including the calendar and the priesthoods'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_ItRomRg] Starting with Tarquinius Priscus (r. 616-579 BCE), Rome was ruled by a series of kings of Etruscan descent, who could thus draw on the legacy of this powerful and complex culture from north-central Italy (around modern-day Etruria). The Roman king served as chief legislator, military commander, highest judge and chief priest.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_ItRomRg] Archaeological remains found on the Palatine Hill dating to the late 8th century BCE suggest that the king lived in a palace from the earliest times. Rome's relationship with other settlements in Latium, particularly on military matters, were important.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_ItRomRg] The hereditary clan system (gens) formed the basis of the Roman nobility,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_ItRomRg] likely serving first as the king's advisors, although administrative structures gradually became more institutionalized (for example, through the establishment of a formal senate and voting assemblies) throughout the late 8th and 7th centuries BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The city of Rome, with a population somewhere between 14,000 and 57,000 during this period, fared well in military and economic terms. Several kings claimed important victories over nearby settlements in Latium and Etruria, expanding Rome's sphere of influence and establishing economic connections throughout central Italy. The Via Salaria ('salt road') and the Sacra Via in Rome were in existence from the beginning of the Roman Kingdom[MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_ItRomRg] - although at this time the roads would not have been paved. A port known as Caere was situated 50 kilometres northwest of Rome.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_ItRomRg] The first bridge, thought to be the Pons Sublicius, may have been built in 642 BCE under Ancus Marcius.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_ItRomRg] Roman kings also reclaimed marshland and carried out drainage works.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_ItRomRg]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 1,000 ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]By end of the Regal Period, Rome held about one third, or more, of the area of Latium Vetas. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"as a consequence of Rome's urban development during the sixth century, which involved increased economic activity, a rise in population from growth and incorporation of foreigners as new citizens, and the increase in the territorial extent of the Roman state, a new military organization was introduced to take advantage of these economic, demographic, and geographical changes, and the result was a hoplite phalanx recruited from new territorial districts called tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_ItRomRg]
♠ Polity Population ♣ [20,000-50,000] ♥20,000-25,000 (Beloch). 40,000-50,000 (De Martino). 35,000 at most (Ampolo). 25,000-40,000 (Cornell). [MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_ItRomRg]Previous estimate: {[20,000-30,000]; [40,000-50,000]}[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"as a consequence of Rome's urban development during the sixth century, which involved increased economic activity, a rise in population from growth and incorporation of foreigners as new citizens, and the increase in the territorial extent of the Roman state, a new military organization was introduced to take advantage of these economic, demographic, and geographical changes, and the result was a hoplite phalanx recruited from new territorial districts called tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_ItRomRg]

Can reasonably infer population was less than 100,000 in 600 BCE. In the 150 years between c700 BCE and 550 BCE Rome acquired c200ha. On the basis of urban area, and a roughly proportional decrease based on Modelksi's 509 BCE estimate (we should remember that as cities acquire more area they generally acquire greater population density), we could infer a population in the region of 15,000-25,000 for 700 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The highest officers in the Roman military system were not professionals. Not until 406 BCE did "Romans introduce pay for military service." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_ItRomRg] This is the earliest possible start date for professional soldiers.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Urban area of Rome[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] early 8th Century BCE: 50 ha. 2500-10,000 using an estimate of 50-200 people per hectare.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] late 8th Century BCE: 80 ha. 4000-16,000 using an estimate of 50-200 people per hectare. Previous estimate: 15,000-20,000.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] mid 6th Century: 285 ha. 14,250-57,000 using an estimate of 50-200 people per hectare.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Previous estimates: [4,000-16,000]: 700 BCE; [60,000-90,000]: 600 BCE

♠ Administrative levels ♣ 3 ♥Before the Roman Principate there was no formal bureaucracy. The old Roman treasury - the aerarium Saturni - was housed in the basement of the Temple of Saturn [MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_ItRomRg] The state treasury of the Roman Republic was kept in the custody of the priesthood inside the temple of Saturn, and was managed by elected aristocratic officials called quaestors.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_ItRomRg] Work on this temple is only thought to have been begun by the last king of the Regal Period, Lucius Tarquinius Superbus. During the Roman Kingdom, therefore, the treasury must have been held somewhere else; one might speculate within the court of the monarch. King Servius Tullius (578-535 BCE) - presumably with the resources of his court - may have used this to administer the first census of Rome.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. King[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "while the urban community at Rome may have begun to develop a distinct, community-based identity from the eighth century onwards, the gentilicial elite of Rome, even as late as the early sixth century, would probably be best characterized as simply 'Latin,' or possibily even 'central Italian'. The presence of this pan-central Italian gentilicial aristocracy would have had a dramatic impact on how Rome interracted with other Latin settlements as it may have blurred man of the assumed settlement-based divisions, particularly with regard to military matters, which seem to have been almost entirely under the purview of the more mobile gentilicial elite."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "After Romulus, the position of king was held by men of Sabine, Latin and Etruscan extraction. The kingship was not hereditary."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]_Central government_[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Senate and an assembly.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] from tradition we can "infer an early elective monarchy, the king being elective by vote of the heads of all the Roman families; (i.e., voting under the form of suffrage known as the comitia curiata), and having as merely advisory council, the Senate."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥
♠ Professional military officers ♣ absent ♥ The highest officers in the Roman military system were not professionals.
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent ♥ Not until 406 BCE did "Romans introduce pay for military service." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_57_ItRomRg] This is the earliest possible start date for professional soldiers.
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ inferred present ♥ Early Roman cults were funded by regular public offerings, large individual donations, and payment for services. The hierarchy could also profit from land ownership. Professionalism of the priesthood likely pre-dates the Roman era as similar patterns are evident in Greek and Egyptian civilization. When the state provided gifts, it was often in the form of a lavish construction, such as a new temple. Examples: priests of Isis were "full-time religious professionals" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_58_ItRomRg]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ absent ♥ Before the Roman Principate there was no formal bureaucracy. The old Roman treasury - the aerarium Saturni - was housed in the basement of the Temple of Saturn [MAJIDBENAM_REF_59_ItRomRg] The state treasury of the Roman Republic was kept in the custody of the priesthood inside the temple of Saturn, and was managed by elected aristocratic officials called quaestors.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_60_ItRomRg] Work on this temple is only thought to have been begun by the last king of the Regal Period, Lucius Tarquinius Superbus. During the Roman Kingdom, therefore, the treasury must have been held somewhere else; one might speculate within the court of the monarch. King Servius Tullius (578-535 BCE) - presumably with the resources of his court - may have used this to administer the first census of Rome.
♠ Source of support ♣ none ♥ Bureaucrats came from the aristocratic class and were not paid.
♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ There was no examination system.
♠ Merit promotion ♣ absent ♥ Roman administration was typically formed out of a class of hereditary aristocrats. Within the army, distinctions between classes of legionary and distinctions between age and experience were not eliminated until Marius in 105 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_61_ItRomRg]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ The first senate building, the Curia Hostilia, existed from about 600 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_62_ItRomRg] The first paving of the Roman Forum occurred around 575-625 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_63_ItRomRg] The first coin minted in Rome occurred about 269 BCE (one in Neapolis produced coins slightly earlier, around 281 BCE) and the first state archives was created in 78 BCE. Other possible buildings include: granaries and storehouses.

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ uncertain_present_absent: 716-600 BCE; inferred present: 600-509 BCE ♥ A formal legal code was first founded in the Twelve Tables of 450-449 BCE. Law thereafter was based on precedent. Our sources of knowledge of Roman law include the forensic speeches of Cicero; the Institutes of Gaius textbook (from 160 CE); and, much later, the sixth century CE Corpus Ius Civilis of Justinian. Wax tablets and papyri (contracts and wills etc.) also provide information on Roman law. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_64_ItRomRg] However, before this time restrictions on funerary extravagance, from the start of the 6th century, may suggest the Twelve Tables laws (of the Early Republic) codified an existing body of law and legal practices. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_65_ItRomRg] At which time in the history of the polity did it become present?
♠ Judges ♣ absent ♥ Professional judges did not exist until the Roman Dominate although at that time their precise role vis-a-vis that of Imperial officials is a matter of debate.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_66_ItRomRg] Before this time there were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.
♠ Courts ♣ absent ♥ During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_67_ItRomRg] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[MAJIDBENAM_REF_68_ItRomRg] (introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_69_ItRomRg]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman forum. Basilicas were multi-purpose buildings a place for banking and money-changing and town hall activities. The forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom.
♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ absent ♥ Law specialists first existed during the Principate when they commanded fees for their expertise. We know this because Emperor Claudius attempted to "limit the fees of advocates, which had become intolerably heavy" to protect "women and other helpless litigants from the rapacity of their lawyers." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_70_ItRomRg] The first law school in Rome, for persons who wished to pursue career in the Imperial civil service, was established late second century CE. "Professional" lawyers replaced orators during the Roman Dominate period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_71_ItRomRg]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥
♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Forum: meeting-place, market and civic centre. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_72_ItRomRg] Sewers: Cloaca Maxima, probably credited to both Tarquins, Priscus and Superbus. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_73_ItRomRg] Reclaimed marshland. Drainage works. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_74_ItRomRg]
♠ irrigation systems ♣ inferred absent ♥ Possibly unnecessary within Italy at this time due to sufficient rainfall. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_75_ItRomRg]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ absent ♥ A pipe network that connects the drinking water to individual settlements is not known to exist / not thought to be present. First aqueduct commissioned by Appius Claudius Caecus 312 BCE. "The wall, made from blocks of volcanic tuff, appeared to have been built to channel water from an aquifer under the Capitoline hill" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_ItRomRg]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Cities of Latium were Hellenistic (Hellenisation of Latium beginning in the 8th Century [MAJIDBENAM_REF_77_ItRomRg]): walls, streets, market places, temples, monumental buildings.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_ItRomRg] [MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_ItRomRg] The multi-function forum building also functioned as a marketplace.
♠ food storage sites ♣ inferred present ♥ Stored in the forum building. Rome's mayoral office which supervised the import of grain, dates back to early days of the Roman Republic. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_ItRomRg] "The Republican stages of the Roman attempt to deal with storage problems are to some extent lost, because the material remains of most of the warehouses we have found belong to the Imperial period, but there are some clues." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_ItRomRg] From literary sources [Livy] it seems that the major development of Rome's river port and its attendant warehouses did not take place until the early second century B.C. Earlier the old Forum Boarium and Forum Holitorium in the centre of Rome seem to have coped with the main flow of imports which had probably come down the Tiber from the Italian hills." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_ItRomRg]
♠ Symbolic building ♣ present ♥ Monumental architecture present from end of 7th Century. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_ItRomRg] 580 BCE monumental sancturary. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_ItRomRg]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ Circus. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_ItRomRg]
♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ uncertain_absent_present ♥ Rome's first elementary school dates to 234 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_ItRomRg] The first private libraries consisted of books looted from Greeks, such as the Library of Lucullus (118-56 BCE). In 44 BCE Caesar commissioned the first public library which was completed under Pollio in 39 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_87_ItRomRg] There was a building for the state archives called the Tabularium constructed 78 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_ItRomRg] "In the developed Republic ... some important colleges of priests maintained books of precedents; the senate's part decrees could be consulted in written form."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_89_ItRomRg]
♠ Roads ♣ inferred present ♥ The Via Salaria, “salt road,” and the Sacra Via in Rome, were in existence from the beginning of the Roman Kingdom. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_90_ItRomRg] The first paved road was the probably the Appian Way which dates to 312 BCE. In about 450 BCE the laws of the Twelve Tables, dated to approximately 450 BCE, issued regulations for the dimensions of roads. So at least from 450 BCE the pre-paved roads had maintenance work done of them. Due to the importance of the "salt road", however, it is likely this mud track had maintenance work during the Roman Kingdom.
♠ Bridges ♣ present ♥ The first bridge thought to be the Pons Sublicius possibly in built 642 BCE under Ancus Marcius.
♠ Canals ♣ absent ♥ The first canal is thought to have been built by Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (consul 187 BCE) to drain the lower Po region.
♠ Ports ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ There was a port known as Caere 50km north west of Rome during the Roman Kingdom. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_91_ItRomRg] A port is thought to have been built under Ancus Marcius. However, another source says: "The port of Cosa, the earliest Roman port thus far known, was founded in 273 B.C." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_92_ItRomRg]

Information[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ Roman weights and measures originated in Ancient Greek weights and measures which suggests first present with the Roman Kingdom. The Roman foot was called the pes monetalis and the "standard measure was kept in the temple of Juno Moneta at Rome." The pace and mile were used for road distances. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_94_ItRomRg]
♠ Area ♣ present ♥ Roman weights and measures originated in Ancient Greek weights and measures which suggests first present with the Roman Kingdom. Romans measured area in square actus (actus quadratus or acnua).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_ItRomRg]
♠ Volume ♣ present ♥ Roman weights and measures originated in Ancient Greek weights and measures which suggests first present with the Roman Kingdom. The Romans had wet and dry measures for capacity which could be used for wine and grain. "The amphora ... was the chief unit of liquid measurement, and related to potter vessels whose cubic capacity actually varied." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_ItRomRg]
♠ Weight ♣ present ♥ Roman weights and measures originated in Ancient Greek weights and measures which suggests first present with the Roman Kingdom. The Roman word for weight was pondus and they divided pounds into ounces.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_97_ItRomRg]
♠ Time ♣ uncertain_absent_present ♥ "A sun-dial was the first instrument they had to measure time, brought from Sicily, after the taking of Catana, in the year of Rome 477." Under Quintus Marcus (118 BCE?) Philipus clocks were invented. Five years later Scipio Nasica (111 BCE?) brought into use a water-clock, an invention of Cressibue of Alexandria. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_98_ItRomRg] However, one might have thought the Greeks and the Etruscans would have had some way of measuring time.
♠ Geometrical ♣ present ♥ Roman weights and measures originated in Ancient Greek weights and measures which suggests first present with the Roman Kingdom. "Roman priests were familiar with ancient geometry and chose to design their lettering along the same lines as the old-established nations of the Near East had done." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_99_ItRomRg]

♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Western alphabet developed c800 BCE and by 700 BCE had arrived in Italy. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_101_ItRomRg]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ Salt was used as payment to soldiers from 406 BCE and was an essential commodity with the "Salt Road" being in existence from the start of the Roman Kingdom period.
♠ Tokens ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Precious metals ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Foreign coins ♣ absent ♥ Greek coinage was present in Rome and the first Roman coin was based on the Greek style. Coin production spread from Asia minor, where it originated in the late 7th BCE, "to Greek colonies in southern Italy by 500 BC. These coins came to be made of pure silver formed between obverse and reverse dies. Bronze coins were first minted in the Greek areas of southern Italy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_ItRomRg] However this was after this polity's temporal span, currency not present in this period.
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ absent ♥ Rome produced its first coin about 281 BCE, a Greek-style silver didrachma, minted in Neapolis (and twelve years later coins were minted in Rome.) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_105_ItRomRg]
♠ Paper currency ♣ absent ♥ The Romans did not use paper currency in any period.
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ Greek coinage was available from the Roman Kingdom so debt and credit structures may have become established at this time. Money-changers and bankers were to be found in the basilicas and the Roman forum.
♠ Store of wealth ♣ present ♥ Boxes and chests for valuables were in existence at least from the Roman Kingdom.
♠ Couriers ♣ inferred present ♥ "Before Augustus, Romans wanting to post a letter had to find a courier wherever they could, and work out the arrangements for delivery ad hoc.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_106_ItRomRg]
♠ Postal stations ♣ absent ♥ No general postal service until the Cursus Publicus, established by Augustus during the Principate.
♠ General postal service ♣ absent ♥ No general postal service until the Cursus Publicus, established by Augustus during the Principate.

Military Technologies[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ inferred present ♥ Copper is used to make bronze.
♠ Bronze ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_107_ItRomRg] "Whereas clansmen were best equipped for and accustomed to cattle raids and skirmishes, hoplites were armoured spearmen who fought shoulder to shoulder in a phalanx formation. These citizen-soldiers were now protected by helmet, corselet and greaves, all of bronze, and wielded a long spear and large shield."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_108_ItRomRg]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ Sword blades made of bronze iron being "comparatively rare." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_109_ItRomRg] Used in spearheads.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_110_ItRomRg]
♠ Steel ♣ inferred present ♥ "By the time of the Roman Republic (c.509—44BC), the use of steel in the manufacture of swords was well advanced and Roman swordsmiths smelted iron ore and carbon in a bloomery furnace (the predecessor of the blast furnace)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_111_ItRomRg] However, this source is not very academic, so a better source is needed to be sure.
♠ Javelins ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_112_ItRomRg] "Other weapons included the javelin" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_113_ItRomRg]

♠ Atlatl ♣ inferred absent ♥ RA couldn't find relevant information, but don't appear in book on warfare [MAJIDBENAM_REF_114_ItRomRg]. Expert advice is needed.
♠ Slings ♣ inferred present ♥ According to a military historian (can a Roman expert confirm whether the data applies to this polity?) many ancient armies used slingers. Vulnerable to counter-attacks, slinger units were usually small and used at the start of the battle. Because of the training required to produce and effective slinger they were often hired mercenaries.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_115_ItRomRg] Stones. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_ItRomRg] Perhaps carried by Servian classes IV and V.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_117_ItRomRg]

♠ Composite bow ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in earlier periods.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_118_ItRomRg] RA couldn't find relevant information, but don't appear in book on warfare [MAJIDBENAM_REF_119_ItRomRg]. Expert advice is needed.
♠ Crossbow ♣ absent ♥ Not at this time: "the hand-held crossbow was invented by the Chinese, in the fifth century BC, and probably came into the Roman world in the first century AD, where it was used for hunting."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_ItRomRg] The crossbow also developed after the Syracuse Greek Dionysios I invented a form of crossbow called the gastraphetes in 399 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_121_ItRomRg]
♠ Tension siege engines ♣ absent ♥ In Anatolia siege warfare was mentioned in Old Hittite records.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_ItRomRg] Presumably at this time the catapult was not used? In India, according to Jain texts, Ajatashatru, a 5th century BCE king of Magadha in North India, used a catapult "capable of hurling huge pieces of stone".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_ItRomRg] Marsden (1969) said archaeological records exist before the 4th century BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_ItRomRg] The Achaemenids (c400 BCE?) are assumed to have had the catapult because the Macedonians did.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_ItRomRg] Pollard and Berry (2012) say torsion catapults first came into widespread use in the Hellenistic period 4th - 1st centuries BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_ItRomRg] The Syracuse Greek Dionysios I invented a form of crossbow called the gastraphetes in 399 BCE which encouraged the development of large tension-powered weapons.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_ItRomRg]
♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥ The counter-weight trebuchet was first used by the Byzantines in 1165 CE.
♠ Incendiaries ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Closest reference currently the Ancient Greeks: "Assyrians deployed military incendiaries based on petroleum, to which Greeks now added pitch, quicklime, and sulfur, directing these ancestors of Byzantine Greek Fire against soldiers, siege engines, and ships."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_ItRomRg]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ absent: 716-579 BCE; present: 578-509 BCE ♥ Livy (1.42.4-43.8) and Dionysios of Halikarnassos (4.15.6-19.4) suggest Servius Tullius (r. c.579 - c.534 BCE) created a citizen army which included garrisoned forces. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_162_ItRomRg]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥ Soldiers provided their own equipment.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_163_ItRomRg]
♠ Total army ♣ 3,000: 716-579 BCE; 6,000: 578-509 BCE ♥ Army size doubled from 3,000 (1,000 per tribe) to 6,000 under Servius Tullius (578-534 BCE). [MAJIDBENAM_REF_164_ItRomRg]
♠ Professional army ♣ 0 ♥ Not until 406 BCE did "Romans introduce pay for military service." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_165_ItRomRg] This is the earliest possible start date for professional soldiers.
♠ Largest fielded army ♣ [200-300]: 716-579 BCE; [2,000-3,000]: 578-509 BCE ♥ Estimates.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]200-300 for pre-Hoplite period 716-579 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Pitched battles were fought by little more than warbands formed by a warrior aristocrat, his kinsmen, friends and clients... We cannot know the size and maintenance of such forces for sure from the evidence available to us, but it is unlikely that numbers were large. Though friends, neighbours and clients served to extend the natural limits of a clan, these warbands could not have exceeded a few hundred men at most, and in most cases numbered far less, because of the economic and logistical constraints inherent in the subsistence-type economy (based on cultivation and animal husbandry, augmented by hunting) over which Romulus’ village presided." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_166_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]2000-3000? 578-509 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Hoplite was equipment used from about 600 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_167_ItRomRg]Stele from[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Whereas clansmen were best equipped for and accustomed to cattle raids and skirmishes, hoplites were armoured spearmen who fought shoulder to shoulder in a phalanx formation. These citizen-soldiers were now protected by helmet, corselet and greaves, all of bronze, and wielded a long spear and large shield."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_168_ItRomRg]
♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ domestic; conscription ♥Livy (1.42.4-43.8) and Dionysios of Halikarnassos (4.15.6-19.4) suggest Servius Tullius (r. c.579 - c.534 BCE) created a citizen army. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_169_ItRomRg]
♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_170_ItRomRg] Hoplite was equipment used from about 600 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_171_ItRomRg]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥
♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ "According to the story Tarquinius Superbus was overthrown in 509 Be by a group of aristocrats who set up a republican government under two elected annual magistrates, the consuls. [...] Curiously enough the leaders of the revolt were themselves close relatives of the tyrant (cf. above, p. 123). [...] The king, who was conducting a war against Ardea, hastened back to Rome on hearing news of the coup, but found the gates barred against him; meanwhile the leaders of the revolt went to Ardea and won over the army, which expelled the king's sons. Brutus and Tarquinius Collatinus became the first consuls, and Brutus made the people swear never again to tolerate a king and to punish with death anyone who tried to restore the monarchy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_172_ItRomRg]
♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ "According to the story Tarquinius Superbus was overthrown in 509 Be by a group of aristocrats who set up a republican government under two elected annual magistrates, the consuls. [...] Curiously enough the leaders of the revolt were themselves close relatives of the tyrant (cf. above, p. 123). [...] The king, who was conducting a war against Ardea, hastened back to Rome on hearing news of the coup, but found the gates barred against him; meanwhile the leaders of the revolt went to Ardea and won over the army, which expelled the king's sons. Brutus and Tarquinius Collatinus became the first consuls, and Brutus made the people swear never again to tolerate a king and to punish with death anyone who tried to restore the monarchy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_173_ItRomRg] "It is also clear, as we have seen, that [Servius Tullius] obtained the throne by means of an illegal seizure of power, something that our sources are unable to conceal, however much they tried to mitigate its implications."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_174_ItRomRg]
♠ Popular uprisings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Previously coded "unknown" with the following text as support: "Not known how Servia [sic] Tullius came to power." However, though it is true that there are a number of uncertainties regarding Servius Tullius' origins and how he came to power, nothing suggests that it was through "popular uprising", which our codebook defines as a "broadly based rebellion in which commoners (non-elites) are the major component". Instead, it seems that Servius Tullius seized power through a coup[MAJIDBENAM_REF_175_ItRomRg] in which the commoners weren't involved--indeed, "Cicero and Livy say, in virtually identical words, that he was the first to rule without a vote from the people".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_176_ItRomRg]
♠ Military revolts ♣ inferred absent ♥ Only known internal conflicts are the expulsion of the Tarquins[MAJIDBENAM_REF_177_ItRomRg] and, perhaps, Servius Tullius' coup,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_178_ItRomRg] both of which were intra-elite conflicts. Previously coded as "absent" with "no established military to revolt", when in fact "the military organization of the thirty curiae and three archaic tribes can perhaps best be dated to the period of Rome's early unification, during the second half of the seventh century B.C".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_179_ItRomRg]
♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ absent ♥ Rome at this time was a city-state. No regions.
♠ rural succession ♣ absent ♥ Rome at this time was a city-state. No rural area and/or non-administrative regions.
♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ present ♥ "According to the story Tarquinius Superbus was overthrown in 509 Be by a group of aristocrats who set up a republican government under two elected annual magistrates, the consuls. [...] Curiously enough the leaders of the revolt were themselves close relatives of the tyrant (cf. above, p. 123). [...] The king, who was conducting a war against Ardea, hastened back to Rome on hearing news of the coup, but found the gates barred against him; meanwhile the leaders of the revolt went to Ardea and won over the army, which expelled the king's sons. Brutus and Tarquinius Collatinus became the first consuls, and Brutus made the people swear never again to tolerate a king and to punish with death anyone who tried to restore the monarchy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_180_ItRomRg]
♠ Violent riots urban ♣ inferred absent ♥ Only known internal conflicts are the expulsion of the Tarquins[MAJIDBENAM_REF_181_ItRomRg] and, perhaps, Servius Tullius' coup,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_182_ItRomRg] both of which were intra-elite conflicts. The sources do not indicate that riots were involved.

♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ inferred present ♥ Latin dialects. "Tradition records that after the destruction of Alba Longa by Tullus Hostilius the Romans absorbed its population and annexed its territory. Tullus' successor, Ancus Marcius, made further gains when he led a series of successful campaigns down the Tiber valley. As a result the towns of Tellenae, Politorium and Ficana were destroyed, and their territories annexed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_186_ItRomRg] "There is some evidence of dialectal variations between the Latin spoken in Rome and that of other Latin communities; one such dialect is perhaps represented by the language of the Faliscans, who lived on the right bank of the Tiber to the north of Veii (see Map 2). It was once generally thought that 'Faliscan' was a separate language, but a recently discovered inscription has shown that some of the morphological peculiarities of Faliscan were also characteristic of the language spoken at Satricum, a Latin community some 80 kilometres to the south. This would suggest that 'Faliscan' is in fact no more than a dialect of Latin."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_187_ItRomRg]
♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ Latin vs Etruscan. "In many ways early Rome was very like its Etruscan neighbours, and had close (though not always friendly) links with them; according to tradition the first war between Rome and Veii occurred under Romulus."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_188_ItRomRg] "The problem is that Etruscan is a completely unknown language; it has no known cognates, and is certainly not Indo-European."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_189_ItRomRg]
♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ inferred present ♥ Latin (Indo-European) vs Etruscan (unknown language family). ♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ absent ♥ Literacy widespread in the region at this time. "It is widely believed that the Etruscans passed on the art of writing to the Latins, but there are good reasons, reinforced by the new find, for thinking that the process of transmission was more complex, and that both Etruscans and Latins learned directly from the Greeks, but also influenced each other.//"A surprisingly large number of inscriptions on stone, bronze or ceramic surfaces survives from archaic Italy. Around 120 Etruscan texts survive from the seventh century BC, and a further 800 or so can be dated to the sixth and fifth centuries. Latium is less well represented (at present around seventy Latin inscriptions can be assigned to the same time-span), but there are special reasons for this, and it would be wrong to assume that literacy was less well established or less widely used in Latium than Etruria."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_194_ItRomRg]

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Iron Age peoples of southern Italy - especially the Samnites - offer further good case studies as they interred their dead in cemeteries often on geologies favouring bone survival. A number of skeletal assemblages have been studied for violent trauma. For example, Paine’s study of cranial injuries among males buried during the sixth and fifth centuries BC at Alfedena concluded that they faced a high risk of meeting a violent end (Paine et al. 2007). Sparacello’s work on the same assemblage makes a compelling, if not conclusive, case that unusually high levels of asymmetric development of the upper limbs among the males in this population resulted from habitual training with the weaponry interred with them (Sparacello et al. 2011)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_206_ItRomRg]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ feriae Latinae ♥ "One can reconstruct a story therefore in which from a very early point in time, perhaps the ninth century BC, peoples of Latium came together at Alba Longa for a festival. [...] The crucial aspect appears to be the sacrifice of a white bull, and the participation in the distribution of the meat from the sacrifice is something which can be taken to characterize the populi Albenses. The area was sprinkled with milk by women. We know also that the festival ends with a huge bonfire, which could portend evil, such as when it forked in two before Pharsalus. Any ritual inaccuracy required a repetition or instauratio; note the last example in 176 where the Lanuvians are required to provide the replacement of sacrificial animal because they had failed to pray for the safety of the populus Romanus Quiritium."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_207_ItRomRg]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_208_ItRomRg]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_209_ItRomRg]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_210_ItRomRg] Thomas Cressy: Changed from 'yearly' to 'not applicable' due to no separation of participants and passive spectators.
♠ Duration ♣ [12-48] ♥ hours. 1-2 days. Though ancient sources do not clearly state how long the ritual lasted, it seems likely that the festival originally lasted only one or two days, and that any additional days were added in Roman times [MAJIDBENAM_REF_211_ItRomRg]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Unknown. Source does not provide absolute numbers. However, it is worth noting that, at the time of the Roman Kingdom, under the reign of Tarquinius Superbus, 47 cities were said to participate. This may help form an estimate.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ In Smith's [MAJIDBENAM_REF_212_ItRomRg] comprehensive review of all that is known about the festivals, ritual specialists are never mentioned. Thomas Cressy: Changed code to 'elites' as the expert, Larson, confirmed that ritual specialist were present by this time.

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ sanctuary rituals (votive offering) ♥ "One of the most striking indications of the formation of the city-state at this period is the development of public communal sanctuaries. Although many of these holy places had been the centres of cult activity from a very early period, it was the sixth century that witnessed a widespread process of monumental development, in some cases leading to the construction of temples" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_245_ItRomRg]. Several of these sanctuary-temples have been found in Rome, but also at sites such as Veii, Orvietum, Lanuvium, Ardea and Satricum, "often with evidence of earlier cult activity" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_246_ItRomRg]. Some sanctuaries were located inside city walls, others outside. Several extra-mural sanctuary-temples have been excavated at Pratica di Mare (ancient Lavinium), and "[o]ne, on the eastern side of the city walls, has yielded large numbers of terracotta offerings in a votive deposit extending from the sixth to the fourth century BC, including over sixty large-scale statues, at least four of them representing Minerva" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_247_ItRomRg]. Another sanctuary-temple at Lavinium yielded a row of thirteen stone altars, suggesting that "several Latin communities each maintained its own altar, just as the Greek cities had their individual treasuries at Delphi" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_248_ItRomRg]. "Although all religious shrines in archaic Italy seem to have been 'international' in the sense that they were offered, and were prepared to accept, dedications from all comers (no doubt on condition that they had money and/or high status), it appears that the extra-urban sanctuaries were established for the specific purpose of attracting outsiders, and inviting other communities to participate in joint celebrations" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_249_ItRomRg]. Bustling trade emporia often developed around extra-mural sanctuaries [MAJIDBENAM_REF_250_ItRomRg].

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ sacrifice to Vesta ♥ Vestal priestesses, or Vestal virgins, made sacrifices to Vesta, goddess of the hearth, each day. The cult of Vesta, supposedly founded by the probably mythical king Numa Pompilius, was probably the most important of all traditional Roman cults, as suggested, most notably, by the fact that it is one of the few cults for which a wealth of knowledge exists, and also by the fact that "any suggestion of an irregularity involving the Vestals or their rituals implied a threat to the city itself" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_306_ItRomRg].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ Vestal priestesses, or Vestal virgins, made sacrifices to Vesta, goddess of the hearth, each day. Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Saturnalia ♥ This festival occurred "on December 17, when the senators performed a mass animal sacrifice at the temple of Saturn, and afterward there was a huge banquet to which everyone was invited. The rest of the week was taken up with non-stop parties and feasts. All shops, law courts, and schools were closed. Normal moral restraints were loosened and everyone was expected to engage in all forms of revelry and fun. This was the only time of year when people were legally allowed to gamble in public. Bands of revelers ran through the streets drinking and shouting 'lo Saturnalia.' "Some of the customs of this festival involved inversions in status. Thus, for one day of the week, slaves were treated as equals, and often at the banquet that day, masters would wait on their slaves and serve them their food. During the festival, everyone wore liberty caps, symbolizing either that for the moment everyone was equal or that everyone was expected to behave with freedom and abandon. Each family would select a princeps, or "leader," of the Saturnalia, who presided over the parties. Often this was someone normally of low status, such as a child. Another custom of the Saturnalia was exchanging gifts. People gave dolls made out of clay to children and wax candles to their friends." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_342_ItRomRg] NB This is a description of how this festival would have been celebrated in much later times--descriptions of earlier versions of the festival could not be found. Moreover, the Vinalia and Parilia festivals would also have featured euphoric elements [MAJIDBENAM_REF_343_ItRomRg], but the Saturnalia appears to be better known.

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Lemuria ♥ "Unlike the Parentalia festival, there seems to be no public celebration during the Lemuria, which makes the festival stand out against the others. [...] Our knowledge about the Lemuria festival is very limited. It seems to be an exorcism of sorts, an expulsion of ghosts from the house. The majority of information about the festival comes from Ovid's Fasti, an elegiac poem which was published in AD 8. [...] The only other information we have comes from inscriptions of state calendars, where the Lemuria is listed, as Ovid confirms, as taking place over three days in May, and is marked as 'N', a dies Nefasti, which prohibited marriage on this day and meant that temples and law courts were closed, and that the Senate were not to assemble. [...] Lemures is another term for the dead. It is thought to denote a 'terrifying and malicious' ghost. [...] Ovid says that the ritual takes place at midnight, and the worshipper, commonly identified as the Paterfamilias, rises. He goes barefoot, and makes a 'sign with his thumb in the middle of his closed fingers', and washes his hands. He then accepts black beans, turns his face away, and throws them away. He says 'These I cast: With these beans I redeem me and mine,' nine times, then washes his hands with water. He clashes bronze pots and says nine times, 'Ghosts of my fathers, go forth'." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_379_ItRomRg].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "The only other information we have comes from inscriptions of state calendars, where the Lemuria is listed, as Ovid confirms, as taking place over three days in May, and is marked as 'N', a dies Nefasti, which prohibited marriage on this day and meant that temples and law courts were closed, and that the Senate were not to assemble."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_380_ItRomRg]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "The only other information we have comes from inscriptions of state calendars, where the Lemuria is listed, as Ovid confirms, as taking place over three days in May, and is marked as 'N', a dies Nefasti, which prohibited marriage on this day and meant that temples and law courts were closed, and that the Senate were not to assemble." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_381_ItRomRg]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The only other information we have comes from inscriptions of state calendars, where the Lemuria is listed, as Ovid confirms, as taking place over three days in May, and is marked as 'N', a dies Nefasti, which prohibited marriage on this day and meant that temples and law courts were closed, and that the Senate were not to assemble."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_382_ItRomRg] Thomas Cressy: Changed from 'yearly' to 'suspected unknown' to fit the inclusiveness code.

♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Source does not provide absolute numbers. It is also difficult to say who counts as a "participating group"--is it the single person in the household who got up and performed the ritual? is it the aggregate of these people, who performed this ritual in different places but at the same time as everyone else? should the wider household count, since it seems likely that they would have been aware of dangerous ghostly presences in the house on the nights in question, and would almost certainly have been woken up by the exorcistic banging of bronze pots? should the whole polity be included, since temples, law courts and the Senate were all closed?
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ The heads of households performed the main ritual acts [MAJIDBENAM_REF_384_ItRomRg]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ RA's guess, to be verified by expert.It is difficult to say who counts as a "participant"--is it the single person in the household who got up and performed the ritual? is it the aggregate of these people, who performed this ritual in different places but at the same time as everyone else? should the wider household count, since it seems likely that they would have been aware of dangerous ghostly presences in the house on the nights in question, and would almost certainly have been woken up by the exorcistic banging of bronze pots? should the whole polity be included, since temples, law courts and the Senate were all closed? Though only one person per household was tasked with performing the main ritual acts, and this while the rest of the household would have been sleeping, it seems reasonable to infer, considering the frightening (and noisy) nature of the ritual, that most household inhabitants would have been aware of the ritual, and emotionally affected by it.

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Jill Levine; Edward A L Turner ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ "Imperium represented one of the fundamental links between the two segments of society in Rome and other Latin settlements, as it gave ritual permission for a particular warlord to act on behalf of the settlement, and therefore represented a social contract, arguably similar to that between the aristocratic warlord with his sodales or clientes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_416_ItRomRg] "the gentilicial elite of Rome, even as late as the early sixth century, would probably be best characterized as simply 'Latin,' or possibily even 'central Italian'. The presence of this pan-central Italian gentilicial aristocracy would have had a dramatic impact on how Rome interracted with other Latin settlements as it may have blurred many of the assumed settlement-based divisions, particularly with regard to military matters, which seem to have been almost entirely under the purview of the more mobile gentilicial elite."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_417_ItRomRg] However, the King could not declare war without the approval of the Senate and Curiate Assembly.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ "Imperium represented one of the fundamental links between the two segments of society in Rome and other Latin settlements, as it gave ritual permission for a particular warlord to act on behalf of the settlement, and therefore represented a social contract, arguably similar to that between the aristocratic warlord with his sodales or clientes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_418_ItRomRg] "The monarch was given absolute power over the people; the Romans called this power imperium. However, the monarch's relationship to the people was seen as similar or identical to the power a father had over his household; in other words, the Roman monarchy was strictly patriarchal. The relationship between a patriarch and his family is a relationship of mutual obligations, and this is how the Romans understood the monarchy. In early Roman society, however, the father exercised incredible authority over the family. The father could sell his children into slavery (or could kill them if he could justify it). This arbitrary power was limited: before a father sold or killed his children, he was required to consult with the family and with the public. While the father was not allowed to kill or sell his wife, he was allowed to divorce her; this was allowed, however, only in the most extreme circumstances. In addition, the father served as the priest of the family. In many ways, the Roman monarch followed this model of power: absolute but limited by the people, their welfare, and tradition. The monarch served as a legislator, as the head of the military, as the head of the judiciary, and as a chief priest to the people. His authority, however, was limited and controlled by a constitution which he was powerless to change."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_419_ItRomRg] "The literary evidence is unanimous on the existence of the rex and the inscription from the archaic cippus beneath the Lapis Niger in the Forum Romanum provides archaeological support for its presence in Rome from at least the sixth century and likely earlier. The power of the rex, particularly as embodied by the grant of imperium..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_420_ItRomRg]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ "Imperium represented one of the fundamental links between the two segments of society in Rome and other Latin settlements, as it gave ritual permission for a particular warlord to act on behalf of the settlement, and therefore represented a social contract, arguably similar to that between the aristocratic warlord with his sodales or clientes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_421_ItRomRg] " The monarch served as a legislator, as the head of the military, as the head of the judiciary, and as a chief priest to the people. His authority, however, was limited and controlled by a constitution which he was powerless to change."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_422_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Right to petition executive ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ King could not declare war without the approval of the Senate and Curiate Assembly. The Senate "judged the legislation and actions of the king to make sure that they accorded both with the constitution and with traditional custom; while the Senate seems to have ratified just about everything the king decided, they still exercised an important check on monarchical power. In this respect, the early Roman Senate served largely the same function that the Supreme Court serves in the United States."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_423_ItRomRg] "The literary evidence is unanimous on the existence of the rex and the inscription from the archaic cippus beneath the Lapis Niger in the Forum Romanum provides archaeological support for its presence in Rome from at least the sixth century and likely earlier. The power of the rex, particularly as embodied by the grant of imperium..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_424_ItRomRg]

♠ Informal impeachment ♣ ♥ ♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ King was the chief priest and the public auspices rituals "formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_425_ItRomRg] Reading of auspices, or interpreting omens to discern the will of the gods, was necessary to legitimise actions of state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_426_ItRomRg] The augurs worked within two defined areas: a "sacral boundary formed by the circuit of the old city wall (pomerium)" referred to as public auspices and "Outside the city (militae, 'in the field'), another set obtained the 'military' auspices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_427_ItRomRg] Other priests and religious authorities under the King had power to offer readings of portents and advice on policy (and advice against policy, such as going to war at a particular time), but indications suggest that this was more advice than censure or critique of regal decisions.
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ King was the chief priest and the public auspices rituals "formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_428_ItRomRg] Reading of auspices, or interpreting omens to discern the will of the gods, was necessary to legitimise actions of state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_429_ItRomRg] In the Republican period the priests "who interpreted the rules surrounding the auspices" were called augurs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_430_ItRomRg] The augurs worked within two defined areas: a "sacral boundary formed by the circuit of the old city wall (pomerium)" referred to as public auspices and "Outside the city (militae, 'in the field'), another set obtained the 'military' auspices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_431_ItRomRg]

♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ King was the chief priest and the public auspices rituals "formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_447_ItRomRg] Reading of auspices, or interpreting omens to discern the will of the gods, was necessary to legitimise actions of state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_448_ItRomRg] In the Republican period the priests "who interpreted the rules surrounding the auspices" were called augurs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_449_ItRomRg] The augurs worked within two defined areas: a "sacral boundary formed by the circuit of the old city wall (pomerium)" referred to as public auspices and "Outside the city (militae, 'in the field'), another set obtained the 'military' auspices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_450_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Customary mechanism ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ "After Romulus, the position of king was held by men of Sabine, Latin and Etruscan extraction. The kingship was not hereditary."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_451_ItRomRg] from tradition we can "infer an early elective monarchy, the king being elective by vote of the heads of all the Roman families; (i.e., voting under the form of suffrage known as the comitia curiata), and having as merely advisory council, the Senate."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_452_ItRomRg] "literary evidence portrays the Roman office of rex as open to a wide range of individuals from various locations and groups, with the primary requirement evidently being personal power and prestige."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_453_ItRomRg] "the tradition is unanimous that the office of rex was not based on direct descent, but rather immediate power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_454_ItRomRg] "strong tradition of power being linked to an individual's ability in martial pursuits, even within the inheritance structure of a gens, suggests that this may have also been the rule among the gentilicial elite more generally, with the position of rex, and the fundamental right to power, being granted in part by the simple ability to seize it"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_455_ItRomRg]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ present ♥ from tradition we can "infer an early elective monarchy, the king being elective by vote of the heads of all the Roman families; (i.e., voting under the form of suffrage known as the comitia curiata), and having as merely advisory council, the Senate."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_456_ItRomRg] "literary evidence portrays the Roman office of rex as open to a wide range of individuals from various locations and groups, with the primary requirement evidently being personal power and prestige."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_457_ItRomRg] Senate responsible for finding new king. An "Interrex" was appointed for the time between kings.

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥the 753-509 BCE period saw the increasing hierarchization of the Roman polity. An apparent feature of this social process in Rome "and Latium more generally" was the emergence of "two distinct groups"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_466_ItRomRg]: a "gens-based aristocracy and the settled, proto-urban and urban populations".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_467_ItRomRg] The settled population initiated developments as it was likely present before the mobile, tribal aristocracy: its curiate system of government is thought to have "represented the archaic urban settlement of Rome".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_468_ItRomRg] Some consider early Rome similar to a Greek polis[MAJIDBENAM_REF_469_ItRomRg] but this Hellenistic influence should not be confused with a (presumably separate) "Hellenization of Rome" which occurred in the late fourth, early third BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_470_ItRomRg] The earliest polity had an assembly with senior magistrates[MAJIDBENAM_REF_471_ItRomRg] and is considered a Kingdom (with local Latin or Sabine kings) lack of evidence means precise details are not available and conclusions are somewhat guesswork.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]An important point is that "the urban community at Rome may have begun to develop a distinct, community-based identity from the eighth century onwards"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_472_ItRomRg] well before it acquired the institutions for which it became famous. Certainly, however, by 6th CE the distinctive Roman polity that was to be the basis of the Roman Republic was beginning to form. The period of Etruscan monarchs, beginning with Lucius Tarquinius Priscus from 616 BCE, appears to represent the time of an additional step-up in hierarchization of the Roman polity, when its two constituent groups (to be known as patricians and plebians) became ever more distinguishable. The first senate building, the Curia Hostilia, existed from about 600 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_473_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The late Roman Kingdom saw the first paving of the Roman Forum around 625-575 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_474_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_REF_475_ItRomRg] as well as large building projects such as the Cloaca Maxima, Circus Maximus and the Temple of Jupiter. Following Etruscan king Priscus, king Servius Tullius instituted some revolutionary reforms which included a new legislative assembly that cemented the power of the aristocracy. In addition to the comitia curiata which was likely "a purely patrician body"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_476_ItRomRg] he "created the form of suffrage known as the comitia centuriata, a method of voting based on property qualification: i. e., in the comitia centuriata all Roman citizens voted; but their votes were grouped in classes according to their property holding."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_477_ItRomRg] In this comitia centuriata, which was probably was the legislature of the late Roman Kingdom (as it was later in the early Republic), votes were "proportionate to wealth."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_478_ItRomRg] In terms of the flora of state institutions in the Late Roman Kingdom we have "an executive, priestly and judicial head, the Rex or king; his cabinet or advisory council, the Senatus, or Senate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_479_ItRomRg] The priesthood and top juditical authority was not at this time separate from the executive. There certainly was no central bureaucracy either - this did not emerge in the Roman world until the Roman Principate when salaried officials worked in the Imperial Bureaux (scrinia)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_480_ItRomRg], although king Servius Tullius - presumably with the resources of his court - managed to administer the first census of Rome. During the Republic period the state treasury was held in the temple of Saturn, controlled by elected quaestors under the supervision of the Senate[MAJIDBENAM_REF_481_ItRomRg] but this building did not exist during the Roman Kingdom so probably neither did elected quaestors. Usually dated to c500 BCE, work on the temple of Saturn is thought to have been begun by the last king Lucius Tarquinius Superbus. If the Roman Kingdom had a treasury it must have been held somewhere else at this time; one might speculate within the court of the monarch.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 0 ♥ Ancient Rome was a city-state. There was no local or regional organization, unless that is conceived of as being within the city e.g. perhaps rulers of the three tribes. Tribes were the basis of political and military organization in middle 7th century BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_488_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"as a consequence of Rome's urban development during the sixth century, which involved increased economic activity, a rise in population from growth and incorporation of foreigners as new citizens, and the increase in the territorial extent of the Roman state, a new military organization was introduced to take advantage of these economic, demographic, and geographical changes, and the result was a hoplite phalanx recruited from new territorial districts called tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_489_ItRomRg]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ absent ♥During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_492_ItRomRg] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[MAJIDBENAM_REF_493_ItRomRg] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_494_ItRomRg]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_495_ItRomRg] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_496_ItRomRg] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ inferred present ♥ “It has been held since the early nineteenth century that the control of property by fathers of families at the time of the Twelve Tables had evolved from a system of ownership by villages (pagi) or clans (gentes)...It is easier to conceive how land owned by a village community, controlled politically by some kind of village council, became subsequently parceled up as private property under the heads of nuclear households.” “The existence of common pastures and woods on the other hand, can be assumed with some confidence” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_542_ItRomRg] “Towards the end of eighth century BC it is possible that a wealthy land-owning class had begun to emerge, who became the elite rulers of the settlement. How this wealth was reckoned in the early days is uncertain. It was probably assessed on property and land, perhaps also on livestock and crops.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_543_ItRomRg]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

EALT:From a glossary of Latin legal terms: stuprum "illicit sex, punishable under the lex Julia de adulteriis. This included adultery (sexual relations between a married woman and someone other than her husband), as well as sexual relations with an unmarried woman or virgin of respectable status."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_550_ItRomRg] lex Julia refers to 23 BCE "in early Roman law, if a woman cohabited with a man for one full year, she was regarded as having entered the man's manus by a kind of usucapio [taking by use]".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_551_ItRomRg]

♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ inferred present ♥ From a glossary of Latin legal terms: concubinatus "a non-legal but long-term sexual relationship, usually between an unmarried man and a woman of lower status with whom iustum matrimonium was either legally impossible or socially inappropriate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_552_ItRomRg]
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ suspected unknown ♥Legal rights are different on the basis of:
♠ Gender ♣ yes ♥ Though the following refers to the fifth century BCE onward, it seems reasonable to infer that the same was the case for this phase of Roman history as well. "As in other early patriarchal societies, the oldest male ascendent within a Roman household was considered to exercise legal control over its other members and property."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_553_ItRomRg] The following text, on the other hand, clearly refers to a change in women's rights in the following period, so it is not entirely relevant here. "[J]uristic innovation contained in the Twelve Tables ... if a woman stayed away from her husband for three consecutive nights every year, she would remain legally independent of her husband by interrupting his usus ... A woman who was in the manus of her husband was basically in the same legal position as her children, who were under the husband's parternal authority. ... If a woman did not enter the manus of her husband, she remained under the paternal authority of her father until he died, at which time she became legally independent. From the time of the Twelve Tables onwards, women who were not under their father's patria potestas or the manus of their husbands were still required to have a legal guardian (tutor) to assist or oversee their legal transactions. This duty was most often performed by a close male relative ... This institution was termed the perpetual guardianship of women (tutela perpetua mulierum), and the Vestal virgins were the only females in early Rome exempt from it (Gauis Institutes 1.145)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_554_ItRomRg] Finally, the source link for the following quote is broken, and the reliability of the source is unclear. "The assembly consisted of all male citizens of Rome".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_555_ItRomRg]
♠ Social class ♣ yes ♥ Existence of slavery.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_556_ItRomRg] The source link for the following quote is broken, and the reliability of the source is unclear. "Only patricians could serve as clan leaders; therefore, only patricians were allowed to sit on the Senate or hold any appointed or elected offices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_557_ItRomRg]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ no ♥ "Unlike many ancient Greek city-states, such as Athens and Sparta, which excluded foreigners and subjects from political participation, Rome from its beginning did not hesitate to incorporate conquered peoples into its social and political system. Allies and subjects who adopted Roman ways were eventually granted Roman citizenship and became fully participating members in Roman society."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_ItRomRg] The source link for the following quote is broken, and the reliability of the source is unclear. ♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ "We may further surmise that, like those in contemporary Greek city-states, the early Roman aristocracy was organized into an advisory or deliberative body consulted by the king. Under the republic, this body was called the senate. Although it often met in temples, its own established meetinghouse was the Curia Hostilia in the Comitium. Once again, given the nature of our evidence, we cannot know how this body was organized under the kings, or what its powers and functions were. Its membership must have comprised the adult men of considerable wealth and social status. Like other relatively flexible communities of central Tyrrhenian Italy, Roman society during the late seventh, sixth, and early fifth centuries B.C . is likely to have been open to horizontal social mobility and even to some degree of vertical social mobility. Consequently, the membership of the early senate is likely to have been characterized by a certain social fluidity."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_564_ItRomRg] However, note the likely existence of slavery[MAJIDBENAM_REF_565_ItRomRg]: enslaved men likely could not access bureaucracy. The kingship was not hereditary."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_577_ItRomRg] "the clear division in Roman society between the gens-based aristocracy and the settled, proto-urban and urban populations, in addition to the dual nature of Rome's 'patrician' and 'plebeian' elements during the early Repbulic (albeit variously construed) hint that Rome, and Latium more generally, was composed of two distinct groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_578_ItRomRg] "while the urban community at Rome may have begun to develop a distinct, community-based identity from the eighth century onwards, the gentilicial elite of Rome, even as late as the early sixth century, would probably be best characterized as simply 'Latin,' or possibily even 'central Italian'. The presence of this pan-central Italian gentilicial aristocracy would have had a dramatic impact on how Rome interracted with other Latin settlements as it may have blurred man of the assumed settlement-based divisions, particularly with regard to military matters, which seem to have been almost entirely under the purview of the more mobile gentilicial elite."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_579_ItRomRg]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ 0-10% ♥ Unclear, but slavery likely existed in Rome before the 5th century BCE (before the Twelve Tables).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_588_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The earliest known Roman code relating to slavery is the Twelve Tables, which dates to approxiately 450 B.C. During the republic and early empire, magisterial edicts, imperial orders, and occasionally senatorial decrees affected the structure, application, and evolution of Roman slave law. Though a few legal texts exist from the first two centuries A.D., no formal and comprehensive compilation of law, including laws pertaining to slavery, appeared before the Theodosian Code (Codex Theodosianus) was assembled in A.D. 435."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_589_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"the clear division in Roman society between the gens-based aristocracy and the settled, proto-urban and urban populations, in addition to the dual nature of Rome's 'patrician' and 'plebeian' elements during the early Repbulic (albeit variously construed) hint that Rome, and Latium more generally, was composed of two distinct groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_590_ItRomRg] no mention of slaves.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]No slavery until fifth century? If there was, number was negligible until warfare.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Slavery in the Roman Republic was fully institutionalized by 450 BCE, and was incorporated into the Twelve Tables legal code (451-450 BCE)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_591_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Mid-4th BCE: "One major change is likely to have been Rome's gradual transformation into a slave-owning society. Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_592_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "That Roman agriculture in the late Republic was heavily dependent on the labour of slaves is well known, but it is not always appreciated that this phenomenon has its origin in the fourth century B.C."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_593_ItRomRg][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Debt-bondage abolished in 326 or 313 BCE due to influx of slaves acquired through warfare.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_594_ItRomRg]

♠ Description of the Normative Ideology ♣ ♥ "The problem is not lack of written material, but how we should interpret and make sense o f that material. For all the accounts we have of Rome's earliest history are found in writers (Dionysius amongst them) who lived in the first Century B.C. or later - more than 600 years after the dates usually given to the reigns of Romulus and Numa. None of our sources is contemporary with the events they describe. Nor could their authors have read any such contemporary accounts on which to base their own: so far as we know, there were no writers in earliest, regal Rome; there was no account left by Numa, say, of his religious foundations. [...] Judged by our own Standards of historical 'accuracy', these ancient accounts of early Rome and its religion are inadequate and misleading; they construct an image of a relatively sophisticated society, more like the city of the first Century B. C. than the hamlet of the eighth Century. Projections of the contemporary world back into the distant past, they are more myth than history. " [MAJIDBENAM_REF_599_ItRomRg] "Recent work, particularly in archaeology, has cast doubt on the idea of an early, uncontaminated, native strand of genuine Roman religion; and it has suggested that, rather than seeing pure Roman traditions gradually polluted from outside, Roman religion was an amalgam of different traditions from at least as far back as we can hope to go. Leaving aside its mythical prehistory, Roman religion was always already multicultural." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_600_ItRomRg] "Much of the evidence for the early history of the Roman gods remains tantalizing. But it is possible to offer a rough outline of their place in the life of Rome: closely involved in the political and military activity of the city, they are seen as forces outside the human Community with whom the man of learning and skill, knowing the rules, traditions and rituals, can negotiate and communicate (and if necessary assuage); the activities of the city's leaders on the city's behalf could hardly be conceived except in the context of such a procedure of negotiation and Joint action; divine benevolence (secured by human effort) was essential to the success of the State; Rome's history in other words was determined by the actions of men and gods together." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_601_ItRomRg]
